<hr /><a href="/wiki/File:New_Zealand_2017_Election_Results_Map_-_Results_By_Electorate,_M%C4%81ori_Electorate,_and_Additional_Member_Seats.svg" class="image"><img alt="New Zealand 2017 Election Results Map - Results By Electorate, Māori Electorate, and Additional Member Seats.svg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/New_Zealand_2017_Election_Results_Map_-_Results_By_Electorate%2C_M%C4%81ori_Electorate%2C_and_Additional_Member_Seats.svg/450px-New_Zealand_2017_Election_Results_Map_-_Results_By_Electorate%2C_M%C4%81ori_Electorate%2C_and_Additional_Member_Seats.svg.png" width="450" height="292" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/New_Zealand_2017_Election_Results_Map_-_Results_By_Electorate%2C_M%C4%81ori_Electorate%2C_and_Additional_Member_Seats.svg/675px-New_Zealand_2017_Election_Results_Map_-_Results_By_Electorate%2C_M%C4%81ori_Electorate%2C_and_Additional_Member_Seats.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/New_Zealand_2017_Election_Results_Map_-_Results_By_Electorate%2C_M%C4%81ori_Electorate%2C_and_Additional_Member_Seats.svg/900px-New_Zealand_2017_Election_Results_Map_-_Results_By_Electorate%2C_M%C4%81ori_Electorate%2C_and_Additional_Member_Seats.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1786" data-file-height="1158" /></a><div>Map of the general election. Electorate results are shown on the left, Maori electorate results in the centre, and the list members on the right.</div></td></tr><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center">
<hr />
<table cellspacing="0" cellpadding="0" style="background:transparent; width:100%;">
<tbody><tr>
<td style="width:50%; text-align:left;"><b><a href="/wiki/Prime_Minister_of_New_Zealand" title="Prime Minister of New Zealand">Prime Minister</a> before election</b><br />
<p><a href="/wiki/Bill_English" title="Bill English">Bill English</a><br />
<a href="/wiki/New_Zealand_National_Party" title="New Zealand National Party">National</a>
</p>
</td>
<td style="width:50%; text-align:right;"><b>Subsequent Prime Minister</b> <br />
<p><a href="/wiki/Jacinda_Ardern" title="Jacinda Ardern">Jacinda Ardern</a><br />
<a href="/wiki/New_Zealand_Labour_Party" title="New Zealand Labour Party">Labour</a>
</p>
</td></tr></tbody></table></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The <b>2017 New Zealand general election</b> took place on Saturday 23 September 2017 to determine the membership of the <a href="/wiki/52nd_New_Zealand_Parliament" title="52nd New Zealand Parliament">52nd New Zealand Parliament</a>. The <a href="/wiki/51st_New_Zealand_Parliament" title="51st New Zealand Parliament">previous parliament</a> was elected on 20 September 2014 and was officially dissolved on 22 August 2017.<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> Voters elected 120 members to the House of Representatives under New Zealand's <a href="/wiki/Mixed-member_proportional_representation" title="Mixed-member proportional representation">mixed-member proportional</a> (MMP) voting system, a <a href="/wiki/Proportional_representation" title="Proportional representation">proportional representation</a> system in which 71 members were elected from single-member <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand_electorates" title="New Zealand electorates">electorates</a> and 49 members were elected from <a href="/wiki/Closed_list" title="Closed list">closed</a> <a href="/wiki/Party_list" class="mw-redirect" title="Party list">party lists</a>. Around 3.57 million people were registered to vote in the election,<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> with 2.63 million (79.8%) turning out.<sup id="cite_ref-PreliminaryResults_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PreliminaryResults-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Early_voting" title="Early voting">Advance voting</a> proved popular, with 1.24 million votes cast before election day, more than the previous two elections combined.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Prior to the election, the centre-right <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand_National_Party" title="New Zealand National Party">National Party</a>, led by Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Bill_English" title="Bill English">Bill English</a>, had <a href="/wiki/Fifth_National_Government_of_New_Zealand" title="Fifth National Government of New Zealand">governed since 2008</a> in a <a href="/wiki/Minority_government" title="Minority government">minority government</a> with <a href="/wiki/Confidence_and_supply" title="Confidence and supply">confidence and supply</a> from the <a href="/wiki/M%C4%81ori_Party" title="Māori Party">Māori</a>, <a href="/wiki/ACT_New_Zealand" title="ACT New Zealand">ACT</a> and <a href="/wiki/United_Future" title="United Future">United Future</a> parties. It was the first election for English as incumbent Prime Minister, having replaced <a href="/wiki/John_Key" title="John Key">John Key</a> on 12 December 2016 and the first since <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand_general_election_1975" class="mw-redirect" title="New Zealand general election 1975">1975</a> where both major parties had leadership changes. The main opposition parties to the National government were <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand_Labour_Party" title="New Zealand Labour Party">Labour</a> (the <a href="/wiki/Official_Opposition_(New_Zealand)" title="Official Opposition (New Zealand)">official opposition</a>), led by <a href="/wiki/Jacinda_Ardern" title="Jacinda Ardern">Jacinda Ardern</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Green_Party_of_Aotearoa_New_Zealand" title="Green Party of Aotearoa New Zealand">Green Party</a>, and <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand_First" title="New Zealand First">New Zealand First</a>.
</p><p>The National Party won a <a href="/wiki/Plurality_(voting)" title="Plurality (voting)">plurality</a> of the seats with 56, down from 60 in 2014.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> Labour made large gains following Jacinda Ardern <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand_Labour_Party_leadership_election,_2017" title="New Zealand Labour Party leadership election, 2017">becoming the party leader</a> seven weeks prior to the election, increasing its representation from 32 to 46 seats. Labour was the only parliamentary party to gain support but a large portion came at the expense of the Green Party, who lost almost half their seats (dropping from 14 to 8) following co-leader <a href="/wiki/Metiria_Turei" title="Metiria Turei">Metiria Turei</a>'s resignation over self-admitted benefit and electoral fraud. The anti-immigration populist New Zealand First Party won 9 seats, down from 12 in 2014. ACT retained its one seat. 
</p><p>National would have had 58 not 56 seats on the election-night count, but when special votes were counted lost one list seat each to the Greens and Labour (Labour was five or six thousand party votes away from getting a second list seat).<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The election saw five parties return to Parliament, down from seven in 2014 and the lowest number since the introduction of MMP in 1996. <a href="/wiki/M%C4%81ori_Party" title="Māori Party">Māori Party</a> co-leader <a href="/wiki/Te_Ururoa_Flavell" title="Te Ururoa Flavell">Te Ururoa Flavell</a> lost his seat of <a href="/wiki/Waiariki" class="mw-redirect" title="Waiariki">Waiariki</a> and, with the party's vote count being below the <a href="/wiki/Electoral_threshold" class="mw-redirect" title="Electoral threshold">threshold</a> of 5%, they also lost their list MP, co-leader <a href="/wiki/Marama_Fox" title="Marama Fox">Marama Fox</a>, and departed Parliament. <a href="/wiki/United_Future" title="United Future">United Future</a> leader and sole MP <a href="/wiki/Peter_Dunne" title="Peter Dunne">Peter Dunne</a> retired from politics during the campaign due to poor polling in his electorate of <a href="/wiki/%C5%8Ch%C4%81riu" class="mw-redirect" title="Ōhāriu">Ōhāriu</a> and his successor failed to win the seat. The party would vote to dissolve less than two months later.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> 
</p><p>Even with support partner ACT retaining its sole seat, the existing National minority government were short of the 61 seats needed to govern, having reached 56 seats, and Bill English declared that the arrangement would not be continued.<sup id="cite_ref-StuffNZ-negotiations_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-StuffNZ-negotiations-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> New Zealand First's nine seats gave it the <a href="/wiki/Balance_of_power_(parliament)" title="Balance of power (parliament)">balance of power</a> between the National Party and the left Labour and Green parties.<sup id="cite_ref-OfficialResults_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OfficialResults-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ABC-FinalTally_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ABC-FinalTally-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> On 19 October 2017, New Zealand First leader <a href="/wiki/Winston_Peters" title="Winston Peters">Winston Peters</a> announced that the party was intending to form a minority <a href="/wiki/Sixth_Labour_Government_of_New_Zealand" title="Sixth Labour Government of New Zealand">coalition government with the Labour Party</a> of 55 seats, with confidence and supply agreement from the Green Party.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> This is the first Government in New Zealand under MMP where the most popular party is not part of the Government. The election resulted in Ardern becoming New Zealand's third female Prime Minister, and Peters being reappointed <a href="/wiki/Deputy_Prime_Minister_of_New_Zealand" title="Deputy Prime Minister of New Zealand">Deputy Prime Minister</a>, a role he had first held in 1996–98. This marked an end to nine years under the <a href="/wiki/Fifth_National_Government_of_New_Zealand" title="Fifth National Government of New Zealand">Fifth National Party Government</a>, and the beginning of the <a href="/wiki/Sixth_Labour_Government_of_New_Zealand" title="Sixth Labour Government of New Zealand">Sixth Labour Government of New Zealand</a>.
</p>
<div id="toc" class="toc"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" style="display:none" /><div class="toctitle" lang="en" dir="ltr"><h2>Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>

<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-4"><a href="#Election_schedule"><span class="tocnumber">2</span> <span class="toctext">Election schedule</span></a></li>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Electoral_system">Electoral system</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Zealand_general_election,_2017&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Electoral system">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Electoral_system_of_New_Zealand" title="Electoral system of New Zealand">Electoral system of New Zealand</a></div>
<p>New Zealand uses the <a href="/wiki/Mixed-member_proportional" class="mw-redirect" title="Mixed-member proportional">mixed-member proportional</a> (MMP) voting system to elect the 120-member House of Representatives. Each voter gets two votes, one for a political party (the party vote) and one for a local candidate (the electorate vote). Political parties which meet the <a href="/wiki/Electoral_threshold" class="mw-redirect" title="Electoral threshold">threshold</a> (5% of the party vote or one electorate seat) receive seats in the House in proportion to the percentage of the party vote they receive. 71 of the 120 seats are filled by the MPs elected from the <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand_electorates" title="New Zealand electorates">electorates</a>, with the winner in each electorate determined by the <a href="/wiki/First-past-the-post_voting" title="First-past-the-post voting">first past the post method</a> (i.e. most votes wins). The remaining 49 seats are filled by candidates from each party's closed <a href="/wiki/Party_list" class="mw-redirect" title="Party list">party list</a>. If a party wins more electorates than seats it is entitled to under the party vote, an <a href="/wiki/Overhang_seat" title="Overhang seat">overhang</a> results; in this case, the House will add extra seats to cover the overhang. The 2014 election saw a one-seat overhang where <a href="/wiki/Peter_Dunne" title="Peter Dunne">Peter Dunne</a> won the <a href="/wiki/%C5%8Ch%C4%81riu" class="mw-redirect" title="Ōhāriu">Ōhāriu</a> electorate when his <a href="/wiki/United_Future_New_Zealand" class="mw-redirect" title="United Future New Zealand">United Future Party</a> was entitled to zero seats under the party vote.
</p><p>The political party or party bloc with the majority of the seats in the House forms the <a href="/wiki/Government_of_New_Zealand" title="Government of New Zealand">Government</a>. Since the introduction of MMP in 1996, no party has won enough votes to win an outright majority of seats. As a result, parties must negotiate with other parties either to form a <a href="/wiki/Coalition_government" title="Coalition government">coalition government</a> or to obtain sufficient <a href="/wiki/Confidence_and_supply" title="Confidence and supply">confidence and supply</a> to operate as a <a href="/wiki/Minority_government" title="Minority government">minority government</a>.
</p><p>Electorate boundaries in the 2017 election were the same as at the 2014 election, with 64 general electorates (48 in the <a href="/wiki/North_Island" title="North Island">North Island</a> and 16 in the <a href="/wiki/South_Island" title="South Island">South Island</a>), and 7 Māori electorates. Boundaries are due to be redrawn in 2019, after the <a href="/wiki/2018_New_Zealand_census" title="2018 New Zealand census">2018 census</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Electoral_law_changes">Electoral law changes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Zealand_general_election,_2017&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Electoral law changes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Electoral Amendment Act 2017 and the Broadcasting (Election Programmes and Election Advertising) Amendment Act 2017 made a number of changes to the conduct of general elections, including:<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>Voters no longer have to complete and sign a new enrolment form if they are already enrolled and notify of a change of address, for example, through <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand_Post" title="New Zealand Post">New Zealand Post</a>'s mail redirection service.</li>
<li>The Electoral Commission no longer is required to send out nominations and polling place information to every voter by post; instead the Commission may use its discretion on how to advertise nominations and polling places.</li>
<li>Polling booths may now use electronic electoral rolls to mark off voters.</li>
<li>Counting of advance votes may now start earlier at 9:00&#160;am (previously 2:00&#160;pm), to take into account the increase in people voting in advance.</li>
<li>The contact information of sitting MPs, such as business cards and signage on out-of-Parliament offices, has been clarified as not constituting election advertising.</li>
<li>Election advertising is now legally prohibited in or near advance polling booths.</li>
<li>Election hoardings may now be erected nine weeks before the election (previously two months), so the first day always falls on a Saturday.</li>
<li>Parties are no longer allocated free airtime on <a href="/wiki/Radio_New_Zealand" title="Radio New Zealand">Radio New Zealand</a> and <a href="/wiki/Television_New_Zealand" class="mw-redirect" title="Television New Zealand">Television New Zealand</a> to broadcast opening and closing addresses.</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Eligibility_to_vote">Eligibility to vote</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Zealand_general_election,_2017&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Eligibility to vote">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>To vote in the general election, one must:
</p>
<ul><li>be on the <a href="/wiki/Electoral_roll" title="Electoral roll">electoral roll</a></li>
<li>be aged 18 or over on election day (i.e. born on or before 23 September 1999)</li>
<li>be a <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand_citizen" class="mw-redirect" title="New Zealand citizen">New Zealand citizen</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand_permanent_residency" title="New Zealand permanent residency">permanent resident</a>, residence visa holder, an Australian citizen or other person entitled to reside in New Zealand indefinitely.</li>
<li>have lived in New Zealand for one year or more continuously at some point</li>
<li>have been in New Zealand within the last 3 years (for New Zealand citizens) or 1 year (non-New Zealand citizens); public servants and <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand_Defence_Force" title="New Zealand Defence Force">Defence Force</a> personnel on duty outside New Zealand, including their partners and children, are not subject to this rule.</li>
<li>not be expressly disqualified from enrolling or voting (e.g., serving a sentence of imprisonment; convicted of a corrupt election practice).<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Election_schedule">Election schedule</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Zealand_general_election,_2017&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Election schedule">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Unless an early election is called or the election date is set to circumvent holding a by-election, a general election is held every three years. The last election was held on Saturday, 20 September 2014.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Governor-General_of_New_Zealand" title="Governor-General of New Zealand">Governor-General</a> must issue <a href="/wiki/Writ_of_election" title="Writ of election">writs</a> for an election within seven days of the expiration or dissolution of the <a href="/wiki/51st_New_Zealand_Parliament" title="51st New Zealand Parliament">current Parliament</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> Under section 17 of the <a href="/wiki/Constitution_Act_1986" title="Constitution Act 1986">Constitution Act 1986</a>, Parliament expires three years "from the day fixed for the return of the writs issued for the last preceding general election of members of the House of Representatives, and no longer." The writs for the 2014 election were returned on 10 October 2014, a day late due to a judicial recount of the Te Tai Tokerau electorate.<sup id="cite_ref-2014_key_dates_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2014_key_dates-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> As a result, the 51st Parliament would have expired, if not dissolved earlier, on Tuesday, 10 October 2017. Consequently, the last day for issuance of writs of election would have been 17 October 2017. The writs must be returned within 50 days of their issuance (save for any judicial recount or death of a candidate), which would have been Wednesday, 6 December 2017.<sup id="cite_ref-sec139_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sec139-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> Because polling day must be a Saturday<sup id="cite_ref-sec139_19-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sec139-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> and two weeks is generally required for the counting of special votes, the last possible date for the 2017 general election would have been Saturday, 18 November 2017.
</p><p>On 1 February 2017, Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Bill_English" title="Bill English">Bill English</a> announced that the election would be held on Saturday 23 September 2017.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> This will be the first election that both major parties, Labour and National are contesting under new leadership since <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand_general_election,_1975" title="New Zealand general election, 1975">1975</a>.
</p><p>Key dates relating to the general election will be as follows:<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Parties_and_candidates">Parties and candidates</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Zealand_general_election,_2017&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Parties and candidates">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Candidates_in_the_New_Zealand_general_election_2017_by_electorate" title="Candidates in the New Zealand general election 2017 by electorate">Candidates in the New Zealand general election 2017 by electorate</a> and <a href="/wiki/Party_lists_in_the_New_Zealand_general_election,_2017" title="Party lists in the New Zealand general election, 2017">Party lists in the New Zealand general election, 2017</a></div>
<p>Political parties registered with the Electoral Commission can contest the general election as a party. To register, parties must have at least 500 financial members, an auditor, and an appropriate party name.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> A registered party may submit a party list to contest the party vote, and can have a party campaign expenses limit in addition to limits on individual candidates' campaigns. Unregistered parties and independents can contest the electorate vote only.
</p><p>The following registered parties contested the general election:<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="MPs_who_did_not_stand_for_re-election">MPs who did not stand for re-election</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Zealand_general_election,_2017&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: MPs who did not stand for re-election">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Electorate-only_or_list-only_MPs">Electorate-only or list-only MPs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Zealand_general_election,_2017&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Electorate-only or list-only MPs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Bill_English" title="Bill English">Bill English</a>, <a href="/wiki/Steven_Joyce" title="Steven Joyce">Steven Joyce</a>, <a href="/wiki/David_Carter_(politician)" title="David Carter (politician)">David Carter</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Jian_Yang_(politician)" title="Jian Yang (politician)">Jian Yang</a> of <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand_National_Party" title="New Zealand National Party">National</a> stood as list-only candidates (as in 2014).<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Andrew_Little_(New_Zealand_politician)" title="Andrew Little (New Zealand politician)">Andrew Little</a> announced in January 2017 that he would not contest <a href="/wiki/New_Plymouth_(New_Zealand_electorate)" title="New Plymouth (New Zealand electorate)">New Plymouth</a>, but would run as a list-only candidate.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Trevor_Mallard" title="Trevor Mallard">Trevor Mallard</a> announced in July 2016 that he would not contest <a href="/wiki/Hutt_South" title="Hutt South">Hutt South</a>, but run as a list-only candidate, with the intention of becoming <a href="/wiki/Speaker_of_the_New_Zealand_House_of_Representatives" title="Speaker of the New Zealand House of Representatives">Speaker of the House</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>Labour announced in March 2017 that the party's six incumbent Maori electorate MPs would not contest the party list but would run as electorate-only candidates, in a challenge to the Māori Party, and to increase the number of Maori MPs in the Labour caucus.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> After the August 2017 leadership change, it was announced that new deputy leader and Te Tai Tokerau MP <a href="/wiki/Kelvin_Davis_(politician)" title="Kelvin Davis (politician)">Kelvin Davis</a> would now contend the party list, as required by the constitution of the party.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Metiria_Turei" title="Metiria Turei">Metiria Turei</a> stepped down as co-leader of the Green Party and after announcing that she would retire from politics at the election, withdrew her name from the party list.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> She appeared in the list of candidates for the <a href="/wiki/Te_Tai_Tonga" title="Te Tai Tonga">Te Tai Tonga</a> electorate.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Results">Results</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Zealand_general_election,_2017&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Results">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Results_of_the_New_Zealand_general_election,_2017" title="Results of the New Zealand general election, 2017">Results of the New Zealand general election, 2017</a></div>
<p>Preliminary results were gradually released after polling booths closed at 19:00 (<a href="/wiki/New_Zealand_Standard_Time" class="mw-redirect" title="New Zealand Standard Time">NZST</a>) on 23 September. The preliminary count only includes advance ordinary and election day ordinary votes; it does not include any <a href="/wiki/Special_vote" title="Special vote">special votes</a>. Special votes include votes from those who enrolled after the deadline of 23 August, those who voted outside their electorate (this includes all overseas votes), hospital votes, and those voters enrolled on the unpublished roll.<sup id="cite_ref-PreliminaryResults_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PreliminaryResults-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>All voting papers, counterfoils and electoral rolls are returned to the electorate's returning officer for a mandatory recount; this also includes approving and counting any special votes, and compiling a master roll to ensure no voter has voted more than once. Official results, including all recounted ordinary votes and special votes, were released by the <a href="/wiki/Electoral_Commission_(New_Zealand)" title="Electoral Commission (New Zealand)">Electoral Commission</a> on Saturday 7 October 2017.<sup id="cite_ref-OfficialResults_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OfficialResults-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Parties and candidates have three working days after the release of the official results to apply for a <a href="/wiki/Election_recount" title="Election recount">judicial recount</a>. These recounts take place under the auspices of a <a href="/wiki/District_Court_of_New_Zealand" title="District Court of New Zealand">District Court</a> judge (the Chief District Court Judge in case of a nationwide recount),<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> and may delay the return of the election writ by a few days.
</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Electorate_results">Electorate results</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Zealand_general_election,_2017&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Electorate results">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:New_Zealand_electorates,_2017.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/New_Zealand_electorates%2C_2017.svg/300px-New_Zealand_electorates%2C_2017.svg.png" width="300" height="212" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/New_Zealand_electorates%2C_2017.svg/450px-New_Zealand_electorates%2C_2017.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/New_Zealand_electorates%2C_2017.svg/600px-New_Zealand_electorates%2C_2017.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="2000" data-file-height="1414" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:New_Zealand_electorates,_2017.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Party affiliation of winning electorate candidates.</div></div></div>
<p>Prior to the election, the National Party held the majority of the electorate seats with 40. Labour held 27 seats, and NZ First, ACT, and United Future held one seat each. Between the 2014 and 2017 elections, one seat changed allegiance: in the <a href="/wiki/Northland_by-election,_2015" title="Northland by-election, 2015">2015 Northland by-election</a>, NZ First leader Winston Peters won the seat off National, after MP <a href="/wiki/Mike_Sabin" title="Mike Sabin">Mike Sabin</a> resigned.
</p><p>Fifty-five electorates saw the incumbent MP re-elected, while another 11 saw a new MP elected from the same party as the retiring incumbent.
</p><p>In three electorates, the incumbent MP was defeated. Labour candidate and lawyer <a href="/wiki/Duncan_Webb" title="Duncan Webb">Duncan Webb</a> claimed <a href="/wiki/Christchurch_Central_(New_Zealand_electorate)" class="mw-redirect" title="Christchurch Central (New Zealand electorate)">Christchurch Central</a> off National MP <a href="/wiki/Nicky_Wagner" title="Nicky Wagner">Nicky Wagner</a>; Labour previously held the electorate continuously from its formation in 1946 until Wagner won it in 2011. After National lost <a href="/wiki/Northland_(New_Zealand_electorate)" title="Northland (New Zealand electorate)">Northland</a> to NZ First leader Winston Peters in 2015, candidate <a href="/wiki/Matt_King_(politician)" title="Matt King (politician)">Matt King</a> claimed the electorate back for National from Peters. Labour candidate and former television presenter <a href="/wiki/Tamati_Coffey" title="Tamati Coffey">Tamati Coffey</a> claimed <a href="/wiki/Waiariki_(New_Zealand_electorate)" title="Waiariki (New Zealand electorate)">Waiariki</a> off Māori Party MP <a href="/wiki/Te_Ururoa_Flavell" title="Te Ururoa Flavell">Te Ururoa Flavell</a>; Flavell's defeat saw the Māori Party without an electorate seat and with the party polling below 5% in the party vote, saw the party voted out of Parliament. Coffey's win also saw Labour claim a clean sweep of the Māori electorates for the first time since the 2002 election.
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Hutt_South" title="Hutt South">Hutt South</a>, National list MP <a href="/wiki/Chris_Bishop_(politician)" title="Chris Bishop (politician)">Chris Bishop</a> claimed the electorate from Labour – the first time the electorate has swung since its creation in 1996. Contributing to the swing was the 2014 boundary changes which saw the Labour-leaning suburb of <a href="/wiki/Naenae" title="Naenae">Naenae</a> swapped for the National-leaning western hill suburbs of <a href="/wiki/Lower_Hutt" title="Lower Hutt">Lower Hutt</a>, and long standing Labour MP <a href="/wiki/Trevor_Mallard" title="Trevor Mallard">Trevor Mallard</a> standing down at the election to contest the party list only. In <a href="/wiki/%C5%8Ch%C4%81riu" class="mw-redirect" title="Ōhāriu">Ōhāriu</a>, incumbent United Future MP <a href="/wiki/Peter_Dunne" title="Peter Dunne">Peter Dunne</a> stepped down after holding the electorate in various incarnations since 1984. Labour candidate and former police officer <a href="/wiki/Greg_O%27Connor_(politician)" title="Greg O&#39;Connor (politician)">Greg O'Connor</a> subsequently claimed the electorate.
</p><p>Overall, National gained one electorate to hold 41, Labour gained two electorates to hold 29, while ACT retained its one electorate seat.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Hamilton_West_(New_Zealand_electorate)" title="Hamilton West (New Zealand electorate)">Hamilton West</a> was considered to be New Zealand's <a href="/wiki/Bellwether" title="Bellwether">bellwether</a> seat.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> Since the formation of the electorate in 1969, the winning candidate was from the party that went on to form the government, with the exception of 1993 when it elected a Labour MP while National went on to form the government (albeit with a one-seat majority). Hamilton West, <a href="/wiki/Maungakiekie_(New_Zealand_electorate)" title="Maungakiekie (New Zealand electorate)">Maungakiekie</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rotorua_(New_Zealand_electorate)" title="Rotorua (New Zealand electorate)">Rotorua</a> were also regarded as bellwethers in the MMP era, swinging with the government at every election since 1996. All three electorates in the 2017 election were won by National candidates.
</p><p>The table below shows the results of the 2017 general election:
</p><p><b>Key:</b>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Changes_in_MPs">Changes in MPs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Zealand_general_election,_2017&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Changes in MPs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Two former MPs (<a href="/wiki/Shane_Jones" title="Shane Jones">Shane Jones</a> and <a href="/wiki/Willie_Jackson_(politician)" title="Willie Jackson (politician)">Willie Jackson</a>) and 31 first-time MPs were elected to the 52nd Parliament, joining 87 incumbents from the 51st Parliament.
</p><p>Following the retirement of Peter Dunne, Bill English (National, list) became the new <a href="/wiki/Father_of_the_House_(New_Zealand)" title="Father of the House (New Zealand)">Father of the House</a>, having served as an MP continuously since 1990. While both <a href="/wiki/Winston_Peters" title="Winston Peters">Winston Peters</a> and <a href="/wiki/Trevor_Mallard" title="Trevor Mallard">Trevor Mallard</a> have served longer than English, their tenures have been non-continuous.
</p><p>23-year-old <a href="/wiki/Chl%C3%B6e_Swarbrick" title="Chlöe Swarbrick">Chlöe Swarbrick</a> (Green, list) became the new <a href="/wiki/Baby_of_the_House#New_Zealand" title="Baby of the House">Baby of the House</a>. She was the youngest MP to be elected to Parliament since the election of the 23-year-old <a href="/wiki/Marilyn_Waring" title="Marilyn Waring">Marilyn Waring</a> in 1975.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Golriz_Ghahraman" title="Golriz Ghahraman">Golriz Ghahraman</a> (Green, list) became the first former <a href="/wiki/Refugee" title="Refugee">refugee</a> to be elected to the New Zealand Parliament.<sup id="cite_ref-refugeeMP_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-refugeeMP-64">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Government_formation">Government formation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Zealand_general_election,_2017&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Government formation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Preliminary election results gave National 58 seats, Labour 45, New Zealand First 9, Green 7, and ACT 1,<sup id="cite_ref-PreliminaryResults_3-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PreliminaryResults-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> leaving no party or obvious coalition able to command a parliamentary majority of 61 seats.  Final results saw the National party lose two further seats, one each to Labour and the Greens,<sup id="cite_ref-OfficialResults_10-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OfficialResults-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> confirming <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand_First" title="New Zealand First">New Zealand First</a> as the election's <a href="/wiki/Kingmaker" title="Kingmaker">kingmaker</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ABC-FinalTally_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ABC-FinalTally-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>  Both the National and Labour parties outlined planned negotiations with New Zealand First in the hope of forming a government.<sup id="cite_ref-StuffNZ-negotiations_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-StuffNZ-negotiations-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>  A National–NZ First coalition would hold 65 seats, while a Labour–NZ First coalition would also need the support of the Green party to form a majority government with 63 seats. National Party leader Bill English ruled out a formal governing arrangement with the ACT Party's sole MP David Seymour,<sup id="cite_ref-StuffNZ-negotiations_9-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-StuffNZ-negotiations-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ABC-FinalTally_11-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ABC-FinalTally-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> though such an agreement existed following the <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand_general_election,_2014" title="New Zealand general election, 2014">2014 election</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>New Zealand First has held the balance of power in previous parliaments and its leader has served in several ministries.<sup id="cite_ref-ABC-FinalTally_11-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ABC-FinalTally-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> Following the <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand_general_election,_1996" title="New Zealand general election, 1996">1996 election</a>, the party joined a <a href="/wiki/Fourth_National_Government_of_New_Zealand" title="Fourth National Government of New Zealand">coalition government with National</a> with Peters as <a href="/wiki/Deputy_Prime_Minister_of_New_Zealand" title="Deputy Prime Minister of New Zealand">Deputy Prime Minister</a><sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Treasurer_of_New_Zealand" class="mw-redirect" title="Treasurer of New Zealand">Treasurer</a>, an agreement that ended when Peters was sacked from Cabinet in August 1998.<sup id="cite_ref-PetersHistory_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PetersHistory-67">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> Following the <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand_general_election,_2005" title="New Zealand general election, 2005">2005 election</a>, NZ First entered into a <a href="/wiki/Confidence_and_supply" title="Confidence and supply">confidence and supply</a> agreement with the <a href="/wiki/Fifth_Labour_Government_of_New_Zealand" title="Fifth Labour Government of New Zealand">minority Labour government</a> that also saw Peters appointed as <a href="/wiki/Minister_of_Foreign_Affairs_(New_Zealand)" title="Minister of Foreign Affairs (New Zealand)">Foreign Minister</a> but not as a member of the <a href="/wiki/Cabinet_of_New_Zealand" title="Cabinet of New Zealand">Cabinet</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-PetersHistory_67-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PetersHistory-67">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Shaw announced the Greens' negotiation team of himself, MP <a href="/wiki/Eugenie_Sage" title="Eugenie Sage">Eugenie Sage</a>, acting chief of staff Tory Whanau, Green co-convenor Debs Martin and campaign staffer Andrew Campbell on 26 September.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> Labour's negotiators will be led by leader Ardern and deputy leader Davis.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Negotiations concluded on 12 October, with "extensive dossiers" provided to NZ First from both Labour and National. The NZ First Board met alongside the parliamentary caucus on 16 October to begin the process of formally choosing a coalition partner.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On 19 October, NZ First announced the formation of a minority coalition government with Labour.  As part of the agreement, NZ First will have four portfolios inside Cabinet and one outside. Winston Peters accepted an offer of the role of Deputy Prime Minister.
</p><p>The Greens will have three portfolios outside Cabinet and one parliamentary under-secretary as a result of a confidence and supply agreement reached between them and Labour.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cabinet_and_ministerial_roles">Cabinet and ministerial roles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Zealand_general_election,_2017&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Cabinet and ministerial roles">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>On 20 October 2017, the names of Labour's 16 Cabinet ministers were announced as Jacinda Ardern, <a href="/wiki/David_Clark_(New_Zealand_politician)" title="David Clark (New Zealand politician)">David Clark</a>, <a href="/wiki/Clare_Curran" title="Clare Curran">Clare Curran</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kelvin_Davis_(politician)" title="Kelvin Davis (politician)">Kelvin Davis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chris_Hipkins" title="Chris Hipkins">Chris Hipkins</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iain_Lees-Galloway" title="Iain Lees-Galloway">Iain Lees-Galloway</a>, <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Little_(New_Zealand_politician)" title="Andrew Little (New Zealand politician)">Andrew Little</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nanaia_Mahuta" title="Nanaia Mahuta">Nanaia Mahuta</a>, <a href="/wiki/Stuart_Nash" title="Stuart Nash">Stuart Nash</a>, <a href="/wiki/Damien_O%27Connor" title="Damien O&#39;Connor">Damien O'Connor</a>, <a href="/wiki/David_Parker_(New_Zealand_politician)" title="David Parker (New Zealand politician)">David Parker</a>, <a href="/wiki/Grant_Robertson" title="Grant Robertson">Grant Robertson</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jenny_Salesa" title="Jenny Salesa">Jenny Salesa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carmel_Sepuloni" title="Carmel Sepuloni">Carmel Sepuloni</a>, <a href="/wiki/Phil_Twyford" title="Phil Twyford">Phil Twyford</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Megan_Woods" title="Megan Woods">Megan Woods</a>. The Labour ministers outside cabinet were <a href="/wiki/Kris_Faafoi" title="Kris Faafoi">Kris Faafoi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Peeni_Henare" title="Peeni Henare">Peeni Henare</a>, <a href="/wiki/Willie_Jackson_(politician)" title="Willie Jackson (politician)">Willie Jackson</a>, <a href="/wiki/William_Sio" title="William Sio">William Sio</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Meka_Whaitiri" title="Meka Whaitiri">Meka Whaitiri</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Green Party announced on 21 October its three ministers outside cabinet and one parliamentary under-secretary slots as <a href="/wiki/James_Shaw_(New_Zealand_politician)" title="James Shaw (New Zealand politician)">James Shaw</a>, <a href="/wiki/Julie_Anne_Genter" title="Julie Anne Genter">Julie Anne Genter</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jan_Logie" title="Jan Logie">Jan Logie</a> and <a href="/wiki/Eugenie_Sage" title="Eugenie Sage">Eugenie Sage</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Campaigning">Campaigning</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Zealand_general_election,_2017&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Campaigning">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Expense_limits_and_broadcasting_allocations">Expense limits and broadcasting allocations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Zealand_general_election,_2017&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Expense limits and broadcasting allocations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>During the three-month regulated period prior to election day (i.e. 23 June to 22 September 2017), parties and candidates have limits on how much they may spend on election campaigning. It is illegal in New Zealand to campaign on election day itself.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>For the 2017 election, every registered party contending the party vote is permitted to spend $1,115,000 plus $26,200 per electorate candidate on election campaigning during the regulated period, excluding radio and television campaigning (broadcasting funding is allocated separately). A party contesting all 71 electorates is therefore permitted to spend $2,975,200 on election campaigning.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> All electorate candidates are permitted to spend $26,200 each on campaigning over and above their party's allocation.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Registered parties are allocated a separate broadcasting budget for radio and television campaigning. Only money from the broadcasting allocation can be used to purchase airtime; the actual production costs of advertisements can come from the general election expenses budget. The Electoral Commission sets the amount of broadcasting funds each party gets; generally the allocation is based on the number of seats in the current Parliament, previous election results, and support in opinion polls. The initial broadcasting allocation was released on 26 May 2017.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> The broadcasting allocation was revised on 23 August 2017, redistributing funds from parties that failed to register in time for the election.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<p>Third party promoters, such as trade unions and <a href="/wiki/Advocacy_group" title="Advocacy group">lobby groups</a>, can campaign during the regulated period. The maximum expense limit is $315,000 for those promoters registered with the Electoral Commission,<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> and $12,600 for unregistered promoters.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> As of  19&#160;August&#160;2017<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=New_Zealand_general_election,_2017&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>, the following third party promoters were registered:<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
