<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Issues">Issues</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Zealand_general_election,_2017&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Issues">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In a January 2017 poll conducted by Roy Morgan Research, 26 percent of respondents named housing supply and affordability as the most important issue facing New Zealand, while another 17 percent named poverty and income inequality.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Education">Education</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Zealand_general_election,_2017&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Education">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>On 27 August 2017, National pledged a $379&#160;million education package. New "digital academies" focused on IT training and similar to existing trades academies, would be introduced for 1,000 year-12 and 13 students at a cost of $48&#160;million. There would also be investments of $126&#160;million to improve maths achievements for primary school students and $160&#160;million to give all primary school students the opportunity to learn a second language if they wished. There would be an expansion of the National Standards scheme that would allow parents to check the progress of their child via their mobile phone.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Labour leader Jacinda Ardern announced on 14 August 2017 that the party had reinstated a 2015 pledge to assist school students to learn to drive and budget. Five driving lessons and the fees for the licence test would be free, and students would be taught life skills and budgeting.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> On 29 August, Ardern announced that Labour would implement its 2016 policy of three years free tertiary education, starting with one year in 2018 and expanding to two years in 2021 and three years in 2024. She also announced a $50 per week increase in the student allowance and student loan living costs.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The ACT party continued to support <a href="/wiki/Charter_schools_in_New_Zealand" title="Charter schools in New Zealand">partnership (charter) schools</a>, with intentions to allow state schools to convert into partnership schools.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> ACT leader <a href="/wiki/David_Seymour_(politician)" title="David Seymour (politician)">David Seymour</a> said on 2 September 2017 that the party would give schools $975&#160;million more, so long as they abandon nationally negotiated union contracts. Schools would be funded $93,000 per teacher in a bulk funding arrangement and would be free to allocate the grant how they wanted, with principals able to decide how much to pay individual teachers. Teachers' pay would be boosted by $20,000 on average, and the payments would reward good teachers and attract "our brightest graduates" to careers in teaching.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Housing">Housing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Zealand_general_election,_2017&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Housing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>According to <a href="/wiki/Quotable_Value_Limited" title="Quotable Value Limited">Quotable Value</a>, residential house prices across New Zealand increased 34.2% between June 2014 and June 2017, from an average of $476,000 to an average of $639,000. In the Auckland metro area, the increase was 45.5% in the same period, from $718,000 to $1,045,000.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>ACT announced its housing policy on 6 August 2017, proposing to scrap the urban rural boundary to free up land for those who want to subdivide and build, claiming that this would create room for 600,000 possible houses.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Green Party leader <a href="/wiki/James_Shaw_(New_Zealand_politician)" title="James Shaw (New Zealand politician)">James Shaw</a> reaffirmed on 22 August the party's intention to introduce a <a href="/wiki/Capital_gains_tax" title="Capital gains tax">capital gains tax</a>, saying that it was "a priority" for the party and a measure "we want to see addressed in a first term of a new government." Shaw said the fact that New Zealand was one of the only countries in the developed world without a consistent capital gains tax had helped fuel inequality between "those who don't own a home and those who now own ten".<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Labour's flagship housing policy was the <a href="/wiki/KiwiBuild" title="KiwiBuild">KiwiBuild</a> scheme, which would aim to build 100,000 homes over 10 years.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> Labour announced on 3 September it would extend the landlords' termination notice period to 90 days (from 42 days), abolish termination without cause, and limit rent increases to once every 12 months (from 6 months). This was in addition to previously announced policies of extending the "bright-line test" for taxing capital gains on residential properties from two years to five years, and abolishing <a href="/wiki/Negative_gearing" title="Negative gearing">negative gearing</a> on investment properties.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Immigration">Immigration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Zealand_general_election,_2017&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Immigration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>According to <a href="/wiki/Statistics_New_Zealand" title="Statistics New Zealand">Statistics New Zealand</a> estimates, New Zealand's net migration (long-term arrivals minus long-term departures) in the June 2016/17 year was 72,300.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> That was up from 38,300 in the June 2013/14 year.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> Of those migrants specifying a region of settlement, 61 percent settled in the Auckland region.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Labour promised to reduce net immigration by about 20–30,000 annually, partly by reducing the number of students enrolled in "low value" courses that were susceptible to being used as a subterfuge for immigration. The party said it would introduce a stricter test regime to ensure employers seek to hire New Zealanders before recruiting overseas applicants, and would require skilled migrants to stay and work in the region their visa was issued for.<sup id="cite_ref-IMMIGRATE_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IMMIGRATE-99">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>National planned to count migrants as "skilled" only if the job they were coming to paid more than about $49,000 a year, but the plan was opposed by employers who said their businesses would be put at risk by the blocking of foreign workers. Immigrants only needed to be paid over $41,859 a year – resulting in about 6000 more workers being able to stay in the country longer. Those earning less would be considered low-skilled and can stay in the country for a maximum of three years, after which a stand-down period applies before they can apply to come back. National planned to introduce legislation in 2018 that would raise the residency requirements for superannuation from 10 to 20 years.<sup id="cite_ref-IMMIGRATE_99-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IMMIGRATE-99">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>NZ First leader Winston Peters vowed to reduce net immigration to around 10,000 per year. Peters said that unemployed New Zealanders would be trained to take jobs as the number was reduced, and the number of older immigrants would be limited, with more bonded to the regions.<sup id="cite_ref-IMMIGRATE_99-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IMMIGRATE-99">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Green Party proposed that migration should be capped to 1 per cent of population growth, but later abandoned that policy due to the perception that the Greens were pandering to anti-immigrant rhetoric.
</p><p>ACT had "long opposed populist attacks on immigrants", according to David Seymour.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> ACT wanted immigrants to "demonstrate they've earned above the average wage in their field" to get residency, and it would put an end to foreigners leaving their kids in New Zealand while paying taxes overseas by implementing an "infrastructure charge" of $10 per day for a maximum of a year for all migrants.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Opportunities Party (TOP) wanted a change so that immigration would not be driven by student visas or reciprocal visitor working visas. The party also wanted to abandon the requirement for highly-skilled migrants to have a job to come to.<sup id="cite_ref-IMMIGRATE_99-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IMMIGRATE-99">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Rail_transport">Rail transport</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Zealand_general_election,_2017&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Rail transport">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>National Party leader and Prime Minister Bill English announced on 6 August 2017 that there would be a $267&#160;million investment in Auckland and Wellington commuter rail, which would include (in Auckland) electrification of the line between <a href="/wiki/Papakura_Railway_Station" class="mw-redirect" title="Papakura Railway Station">Papakura</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pukekohe_Railway_Station" class="mw-redirect" title="Pukekohe Railway Station">Pukekohe</a>, a third line between Westfield and Wiri, and (in Wellington) <a href="/wiki/Double-track_railway" title="Double-track railway">double-tracking</a> the line between <a href="/wiki/Trentham_railway_station,_New_Zealand" class="mw-redirect" title="Trentham railway station, New Zealand">Trentham</a> and <a href="/wiki/Upper_Hutt_Railway_Station" class="mw-redirect" title="Upper Hutt Railway Station">Upper Hutt</a>, and several other improvements.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Labour announced on 6 August 2017 that it would accelerate the building of a proposed <a href="/wiki/Light_rail" title="Light rail">light rail</a> system between <a href="/wiki/Auckland_CBD" title="Auckland CBD">Auckland CBD</a> and <a href="/wiki/Auckland_Airport" title="Auckland Airport">Auckland Airport</a> so that it would be completed within a decade. The plan is part of Labour's wider transport improvements that would include a light rail link to west Auckland, an eventual extension of light rail to the <a href="/wiki/North_Shore,_Auckland" class="mw-redirect" title="North Shore, Auckland">North Shore</a>, a bus network between Howick and the airport, electrification from Papakura to Pukekohe, a third main line between Westfield and Papakura, and other rail and road improvements.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> On 21 August 2017, the party's leader Jacinda Ardern announced a $20&#160;million plan for a passenger rail service linking Auckland, Hamilton and Tauranga. She said that the region would grow by 800,000 people in the next 25 years, and that rail was historically a fundamental travel mode and it was time that it was again.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Green Party announced on 17 August 2017 that it would introduce a passenger rail service between Auckland, Hamilton and Tauranga on a trial basis from 2019. The party had previously said that its policy was to complete the <a href="/wiki/Railway_electrification_system" title="Railway electrification system">electrification</a> of the rail network between Auckland and Hamilton and extend electrification to Tauranga.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> On 25 August 2017, Green party plans for a light rail line between <a href="/wiki/Wellington_Railway_Station" class="mw-redirect" title="Wellington Railway Station">Wellington Railway Station</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wellington_Airport" class="mw-redirect" title="Wellington Airport">Wellington Airport</a> via <a href="/wiki/Newtown,_New_Zealand" title="Newtown, New Zealand">Newtown</a> were announced. The party would establish the line by 2027, and it could form the spine of an extended network north to <a href="/wiki/Epuni_Railway_Station" class="mw-redirect" title="Epuni Railway Station">Epuni</a> via central Lower Hutt and south to <a href="/wiki/Island_Bay" class="mw-redirect" title="Island Bay">Island Bay</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Poverty">Poverty</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Zealand_general_election,_2017&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Poverty">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>During the 4 September 2017 leaders' debate, National leader Bill English committed his party to a target to bring 100,000 children out of poverty within the next term. The National party policy was mainly built around their Families Package, due to be introduced in April 2018, which English said would bring 50,000 children out of poverty.<sup id="cite_ref-povertytarget_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-povertytarget-108">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> They would increase the tax credits for 310,000 families who receive Working for Families, and the abatement level for the tax credits would decrease from $36,350 to $35,000.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>At the same debate Labour leader Jacinda Ardern committed to changing the law to require that every government budget update would have to include a measurement of child poverty.<sup id="cite_ref-povertytarget_108-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-povertytarget-108">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> Labour promised to extend Working for Families to another 30,000 families, by raising the abatement threshold and increasing the tax credits. They introduced the Best Start package that would give $60 a week for each child in their first year and extend paid parental leave to 26 weeks.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> 
</p><p>On 16 July 2017, the Green Party announced at their AGM that they would increase all benefits by 20% and remove all penalties and excessive sanctions to those on the benefit, looking to change the “punitive culture” at MSD.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> They would boost the Working for Families package and raise the minimum wage to $17.75.<sup id="cite_ref-povertypolicy_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-povertypolicy-112">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Māori Party set a target to eliminate all poverty by 2025, introducing a living wage for all workers and making cost of living adjustments to all benefits.<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> The party promised to increase support to grandparents raising grandchildren and expand <a href="/wiki/Wh%C4%81nau_Ora" title="Whānau Ora">Whānau Ora</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-povertypolicy_112-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-povertypolicy-112">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> 
</p><p>TOP proposed an introduction of a universal basic income of $200 a week to all families with children under 3 (or under 6 if adopted), which would replace paid maternity leave. Low income families regardless of employment would get $72 a week and free child care.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>ACT campaigned on implementing lifetime limits of five years for the sole parent benefit and three years for the jobseeker benefit. They proposed cutting Working for Families and paid parental leave for upper income earners.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> On 12 July 2017, ACT deputy leader Beth Houlbrooke made a Facebook post criticising Labour's Best Start package, stating "The fact is, parents who cannot afford to have children should not be having them. ACT believes in personal responsibility, meaning we stand with the majority of parents who wait and save before having children."<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Party_campaigns">Party campaigns</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Zealand_general_election,_2017&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Party campaigns">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="National">National</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Zealand_general_election,_2017&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: National">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>National was campaigning for a fourth term in government. If successful, it would have been the first four-term government since the <a href="/wiki/Second_National_Government_of_New_Zealand" title="Second National Government of New Zealand">Second National Government</a> (1960–72). National revealed the design of its first tranche of party hoardings in early July, featuring leader Bill English and the slogan "Delivering for New Zealanders".<sup id="cite_ref-hoardings0807_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hoardings0807-117">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On 20 August 2017, English announced that the government, if re-elected, planned to build ten new "Roads of National Significance" at a cost of $10.5&#160;billion. Four-laning the <a href="/wiki/Hawke%27s_Bay_Expressway" title="Hawke&#39;s Bay Expressway">Hawke's Bay Expressway</a>, a solution for the <a href="/wiki/Manawatu_Gorge" title="Manawatu Gorge">Manawatu Gorge road</a> (closed after rock falls in April 2017 and a long history of such falls),<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> and a highway from <a href="/wiki/Wellsford,_New_Zealand" class="mw-redirect" title="Wellsford, New Zealand">Wellsford</a> to <a href="/wiki/Whangarei" title="Whangarei">Whangarei</a> were included.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The party announced on 13 August 2017 that it would create a new <a href="/wiki/Boot_camp_(correctional)" title="Boot camp (correctional)">bootcamp</a> for youth offenders at the <a href="/wiki/Waiouru_Military_Camp" title="Waiouru Military Camp">Waiouru Military Camp</a>. English said that there were about 150 "very serious young offenders". The justice minister said that a new Young Serious Offender (YSO) classification would be established for the group.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Prime Minister Bill English and Health Minister <a href="/wiki/Jonathan_Coleman_(politician)" title="Jonathan Coleman (politician)">Jonathan Coleman</a> announced on 19 August 2017 that a new hospital costing more than $1.2&#160;billion would be built to replace <a href="/wiki/Dunedin_Public_Hospital" title="Dunedin Public Hospital">Dunedin Hospital</a>, rather than refurbishing the existing building. It would be expected to open in 7–10 years.<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup> On 21 August, English and Coleman announced a pledge that 600,000 low income people would have access to $18 doctors' visits. The <a href="/wiki/Community_Services_Card" title="Community Services Card">Community Services Card</a> would also be expanded to an additional 350,000 people with low incomes and high housing costs.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The party's infrastructure spokesman <a href="/wiki/Steven_Joyce" title="Steven Joyce">Steven Joyce</a> announced on 2 September 2017 that a National Infrastructure Commission would be set up to help expand and oversee <a href="/wiki/Public%E2%80%93private_partnership" title="Public–private partnership">public–private partnerships</a> (PPPs). The commission would supervise large infrastructure projects, such as the building of new schools, roads, and hospitals, which would be built as PPPs. It was likely that the first project would be the $50&#160;million rebuild of <a href="/wiki/Whangarei_Boys%27_High_School" class="mw-redirect" title="Whangarei Boys&#39; High School">Whangarei Boys' High School</a>, but several other large projects totalling several billion dollars were also being considered.<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Labour">Labour</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Zealand_general_election,_2017&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Labour">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Jacinda_Ardern_at_the_University_of_Auckland.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Jacinda_Ardern_at_the_University_of_Auckland.jpg/200px-Jacinda_Ardern_at_the_University_of_Auckland.jpg" width="200" height="300" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Jacinda_Ardern_at_the_University_of_Auckland.jpg/300px-Jacinda_Ardern_at_the_University_of_Auckland.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Jacinda_Ardern_at_the_University_of_Auckland.jpg/400px-Jacinda_Ardern_at_the_University_of_Auckland.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1825" data-file-height="2738" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Jacinda_Ardern_at_the_University_of_Auckland.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ardern campaigning at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Auckland" title="University of Auckland">University of Auckland</a>, 1 September 2017</div></div></div>
<p>Labour announced it would reverse the tax cuts included in the 2017 Budget and instead increase Working for Families rates and introduce a new benefit for families with children under 3 years old. It also would introduce a winter heating supplement for people on superannuation and benefits.<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On 1 August 2017, party leader <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Little_(New_Zealand_politician)" title="Andrew Little (New Zealand politician)">Andrew Little</a> resigned on the back of poor opinion polling performance. Deputy leader Jacinda Ardern was unanimously elected leader by the party caucus, while <a href="/wiki/Kelvin_Davis_(politician)" title="Kelvin Davis (politician)">Kelvin Davis</a> was unanimously elected deputy leader to replace Ardern.<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup> The leadership change saw a large boost in the Labour Party's support – the party had received $250,000 in donations and signed up 1000 volunteers within 24 hours of the leadership change, according to party secretary Andrew Kirton.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The design for Labour's first tranche of party hoardings was released in early July, featuring both Little and Ardern with the slogan "A fresh approach".<sup id="cite_ref-hoardings0807_117-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hoardings0807-117">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup> After the change of leadership, the new hoardings solely featured Ardern with the new slogan "Let's do this".<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On 26 August, Ardern announced a plan to cut fees for visits to doctors. Community Services Card holders would be charged $8 for a visit to a doctor, teenagers would be charged $2, and under–13s would still pay nothing. The cost for an average adult would fall from $42 to $32.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Labour's finance spokesman <a href="/wiki/Grant_Robertson" title="Grant Robertson">Grant Robertson</a> announced on 14 September 2017, nine days before the election, that there would be "no new taxes or levies" until after the 2020 election. Any changes arising from its tax working group would not take effect until 2021. Robertson's announcement reversed the position taken by leader Jacinda Ardern who had reserved the right to implement changes before obtaining a mandate at the 2020 election, and came as a Newshub-Reid Research poll showed National rising at the expense of Labour. Labour had gone into the previous two general elections with a capital gains tax policy.<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Green">Green</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Zealand_general_election,_2017&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Green">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The Green Party launched its election campaign on 9 July in <a href="/wiki/Nelson,_New_Zealand" title="Nelson, New Zealand">Nelson</a>. Bryce Edwards writing for <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_Zealand_Herald" title="The New Zealand Herald">The New Zealand Herald</a></i> claimed the party's policies announced in the run up to the election showed that: "After years of watering down policies and desperately trying to make themselves more respectable to the mainstream, they have made an abrupt shift to the left". One of the major announcements was the party's new radical welfare reform proposals. Social policy academic and welfare campaigner Susan St John gave the social welfare reforms a "definite thumbs up", pointing to two "breathtakingly bold policies" within the reforms. These two aspects included in the reforms were, one: "sole parents to keep their sole parent support when they attempt to repartner. She is the one to say, not WINZ [Work and Income New Zealand], when she is in a partnership in the nature of marriage". A second aspect would be to make "the In–Work Tax Credit available to all low income families".<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The party also announced as part of its 'Families Package' it would lower the bottom rate of tax to 9%, introduce a new top rate of tax of 40% on those with an income over $150,000 and increase all core benefits by 20%.<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Another policy announced by the party was the proposed introduction of an interim $0.10 per litre excise levy on bottled water. This would be in place until "a proposed working party helped develop a system to charge all commercial water users 'a fair amount'".<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On 16 July 2017, co-leader Metiria Turei publicly admitted that she had not disclosed to <a href="/wiki/Ministry_of_Social_Development_(New_Zealand)" title="Ministry of Social Development (New Zealand)">Work and Income New Zealand</a> that she was accepting rent from flatmates while on the Domestic Purposes Benefit in the early 1990s,<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup> and admitted on 3 August 2017 that she had registered a false residential address to vote for a friend who was running in the Mount Albert electorate in <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand_general_election,_1993" title="New Zealand general election, 1993">1993</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Stuff-04Aug2017_135-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stuff-04Aug2017-135">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup> On 7 August 2017,<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup> MPs <a href="/wiki/David_Clendon" title="David Clendon">David Clendon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kennedy_Graham" title="Kennedy Graham">Kennedy Graham</a> announced that they planned to resign as Green Party candidates for the 2017 election, due to Turei's revelations and her handling of the resulting situation.<sup id="cite_ref-graham-clendon-rnz-1_137-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-graham-clendon-rnz-1-137">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup> Both Clendon and Graham resigned from the party caucus the following day,<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> after the party made moves to remove them involuntarily.<sup id="cite_ref-graham-clendon-rnz-1_137-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-graham-clendon-rnz-1-137">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On 9 August 2017, Turei resigned as co-leader and as a list candidate for the 2017 election, saying that the "scrutiny on [her] family has become unbearable".<sup id="cite_ref-rnz-turei-resigns_139-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rnz-turei-resigns-139">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup> She campaigned in the <a href="/wiki/Te_Tai_Tonga" title="Te Tai Tonga">Te Tai Tonga</a> Māori electorate,<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup> and retired from Parliament at the election.<sup id="cite_ref-rnz-turei-resigns_139-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rnz-turei-resigns-139">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup> Per their constitution, the Green Party will choose a replacement co-leader at the next annual general meeting in 2018, leaving <a href="/wiki/James_Shaw_(New_Zealand_politician)" title="James Shaw (New Zealand politician)">James Shaw</a> as the sole party leader through the election campaign.<sup id="cite_ref-rnz-turei-resigns_139-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rnz-turei-resigns-139">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On 21 August 2017, the party promised free public transport for students and those aged under 19, to be achieved by means of a "green card". Green Party transport spokeswoman <a href="/wiki/Julie_Anne_Genter" title="Julie Anne Genter">Julie Anne Genter</a> said the cost of the card would be $70–80&#160;million.<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The party announced plans on 2 September 2017 to counter pollution by introducing a tax on farmers of $2 per kilogram of nitrate (fertiliser), which it said would raise about $136&#160;million per year. The party would also distribute funds and allow concessions to the agricultural sector in response to declining water quality.<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Leader James Shaw said on 17 September 2017 that the party wanted a capital gains tax, exclusive of the family home, to be implemented in the first year after the election, if Labour and the Greens formed a coalition government. Labour had already said that if it became the government, any capital gains tax recommendations made by its tax working group would not be implemented until after the 2020 election.<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="New_Zealand_First">New Zealand First</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Zealand_general_election,_2017&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: New Zealand First">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>New Zealand First launched its campaign in <a href="/wiki/Palmerston_North" title="Palmerston North">Palmerston North</a> on 25 June 2017. Announced policies included ring-fencing GST to the regions it is collected from, writing off student loans of people willing to work outside major centres,<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup> cutting net immigration to 10,000 per year, retaining the superannuation age at 65, and holding two binding referenda on whether Māori electorates should be abolished and whether the number of MPs should be reduced to 100.<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>New Zealand First also campaigned on increasing the minimum wage to $17.<sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup> They would later increase it to $20.<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On 31 August 2017, party leader Winston Peters announced a policy of relocating the <a href="/wiki/Port_of_Auckland" class="mw-redirect" title="Port of Auckland">Port of Auckland</a> to <a href="/wiki/Marsden_Point" title="Marsden Point">Marsden Point</a> by 2027.<sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup> Peters had vowed in July that a Northport <a href="/wiki/Marsden_Point_Branch" title="Marsden Point Branch">rail connection to Marsden Point</a> at a cost of up to $1&#160;billion was non-negotiable in any post–election coalition between NZ First and either National or Labour.<sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="M.C4.81ori"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Māori">Māori</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Zealand_general_election,_2017&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Māori">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The Māori Party election campaign focused on protecting indigenous rights by providing an independent voice for Māori. The party's <a href="/wiki/Wh%C4%81nau" title="Whānau">whānau</a> based policies focuses on obtaining affordable housing to help Māori in low-wage jobs<sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup>, strengthening employment-support for Māori beneficiaries<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup> and making Te Reo Māori and Māori history core curriculum subjects in all schools up to year 10.<sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup> 
</p><p>The party's policies on <a href="/wiki/Rangatiratanga" class="mw-redirect" title="Rangatiratanga">rangatiratanga</a> focused on combating climate change in the Pacific to alleviate environmental destruction of our neighbouring Pacific nation and scholarships for Māori and Pasifika students to strengthen recruitment and retention of these students in tertiary institutions.<sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The party's policies on <a href="/wiki/K%C4%81wanatanga" class="mw-redirect" title="Kāwanatanga">kāwanatanga</a> focused on protecting freshwater as <a href="/wiki/T%C4%81onga" class="mw-redirect" title="Tāonga">tāonga</a><sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup> and growing iwi economic resources.<sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup> They released a policy for a new rail scheme called IwiRail<sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup> which they said would open up the regions to freight and tourism. Since the <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand_general_election,_2008" title="New Zealand general election, 2008">2008 general election</a>, the party provided parliamentary support to the <a href="/wiki/Fifth_National_Government_of_New_Zealand" title="Fifth National Government of New Zealand">Fifth National Government</a>. 
</p><p>In previous elections their policies included improving public transport with fewer emissions, giving tax breaks to lower income earners, taking <a href="/wiki/Goods_and_Services_Tax_(New_Zealand)" title="Goods and Services Tax (New Zealand)">GST</a> off food products, and banning the use of controversial <a href="/wiki/Sodium_fluoroacetate" title="Sodium fluoroacetate">1080 poison</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="ACT">ACT</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Zealand_general_election,_2017&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: ACT">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Since the <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand_general_election,_2008" title="New Zealand general election, 2008">2008 general election</a>, the party provided parliamentary support to the <a href="/wiki/Fifth_National_Government_of_New_Zealand" title="Fifth National Government of New Zealand">Fifth National Government</a>.
</p><p>ACT announced policies including tax cuts, with the top personal tax bracket reducing from 33% to 25%,<sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:No_original_research#Primary,_secondary_and_tertiary_sources" title="Wikipedia:No original research"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable secondary sources. (August 2017)">non-primary source needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> and raising the age of superannuation<sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup> from 65 to 67 gradually every 2 months starting in 2020.<sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:No_original_research#Primary,_secondary_and_tertiary_sources" title="Wikipedia:No original research"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable secondary sources. (August 2017)">non-primary source needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="The_Opportunities_Party_.28TOP.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Opportunities_Party_(TOP)">The Opportunities Party (TOP)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Zealand_general_election,_2017&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: The Opportunities Party (TOP)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In taxation, the party proposed to deem a minimum rate of return for all assets (including housing, land and business assets) and charge a tax on it. At the same time, reduce income tax rates so that the total tax take would remain unchanged. The changes would be done gradually to ensure house prices remain stable while incomes grow.<sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup> The party considered the existing tax regime to favour owners of capital and to over-tax wage earners, to favour home-owners and to disadvantage those who rent their home, and to encourage investment in real estate rather than productive businesses.<sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>They would tighten immigration laws and shift the focus to attracting highly skilled migrants. Criteria for immigrants would involve demonstrating they can help improve the living standards of all New Zealanders, limiting net immigration to 1% population growth per annum (i.e. 47,900 based on June 2017 population),<sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup> and making access to permanent residency harder and longer.<sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The party's founder, Gareth Morgan, announced plans to almost halve the number of prison inmates by 2027. Morgan said "New Zealand has some of the world's worst and most outdated criminal justice policies", and to reduce the prison population by 40%, the party wanted to scrap the 'three strikes' law, extend eligibility for the Youth Court to offenders under 20, and increase funding for restorative justice.<sup id="cite_ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="United_Future">United Future</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Zealand_general_election,_2017&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: United Future">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>On 21 August 2017, United Future leader and sole MP <a href="/wiki/Peter_Dunne" title="Peter Dunne">Peter Dunne</a> announced that he was quitting politics at the election, citing recent polling and his perception that there was a mood for change in his seat of <a href="/wiki/%C5%8Ch%C4%81riu_(New_Zealand_electorate)" title="Ōhāriu (New Zealand electorate)">Ōhāriu</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup> United Future's candidate for the Botany electorate took over as leader shortly after, promising to move his party towards Labour because of its stance on social issues.<sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Major_debates">Major debates</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Zealand_general_election,_2017&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Major debates">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Television New Zealand (TVNZ) hosted three television leaders' debates; two between the National and Labour leaders, and one where the leaders of the secondary and minor parties were also invited. The two National-Labour debates were to be moderated by <a href="/wiki/Newstalk_ZB" title="Newstalk ZB">Newstalk ZB</a> and <i><a href="/wiki/Seven_Sharp" title="Seven Sharp">Seven Sharp</a></i> host <a href="/wiki/Mike_Hosking" title="Mike Hosking">Mike Hosking</a>. Hosking withdrew from moderating the multi-party debate due to illness; the debate was instead moderated by <i>1 News</i> political editor Corin Dann.<sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup> It also hosted an online debate focusing on young voters and youth issues, moderated by <i><a href="/wiki/Breakfast_(TVNZ)" class="mw-redirect" title="Breakfast (TVNZ)">Breakfast</a></i> host <a href="/wiki/Jack_Tame" title="Jack Tame">Jack Tame</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-TVNZdebates_171-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TVNZdebates-171">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>To be able to participate in their multiparty leaders debate, TVNZ requires a party to have an MP already in parliament, or be polling above 3% in the most recent One News/Colmar Brunton poll. Polling at 1.9%, new party TOP met neither of that criteria. Party leader Gareth Morgan filed an urgent judicial review, arguing that as he was polling higher than ACT, United Future and the Mãori party (who all fit the criteria by having at least one MP in parliament), his party had a right to be involved in the debates and TOP's exclusion was problematic. The case was heard at the Auckland High Court on the 7 September, with the judge ultimately ruling against Morgan.<sup id="cite_ref-TOPdebate_172-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TOPdebate-172">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-173" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-173">&#91;167&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Three_(New_Zealand)" class="mw-redirect" title="Three (New Zealand)">Three</a> hosted one television leaders' debate between the National and Labour leaders on 4 September. The debate was moderated by <i><a href="/wiki/Newshub" title="Newshub">Newshub</a></i> political editor <a href="/wiki/Patrick_Gower" title="Patrick Gower">Patrick Gower</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-TV3debates_174-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TV3debates-174">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Fairfax_New_Zealand" title="Fairfax New Zealand">Fairfax</a> again hosted a debate, the <i><a href="/wiki/Stuff.co.nz" title="Stuff.co.nz">Stuff</a></i> Leaders Debate (formerly called <i><a href="/wiki/The_Press" title="The Press">The Press</a></i> Leaders Debate), between the National and Labour leaders on 7 September. It was moderated by Fairfax's South Island editor-in-chief <a href="/wiki/Joanna_Norris" title="Joanna Norris">Joanna Norris</a> and <i>Stuff</i> political editor Tracy Watkins, and was streamed online.<sup id="cite_ref-stuffdebates_175-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-stuffdebates-175">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Endorsements">Endorsements</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Zealand_general_election,_2017&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Endorsements">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Unlike in some other countries, political endorsements in New Zealand by media and people outside the political sphere are rare.<sup id="cite_ref-183" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-183">&#91;177&#93;</a></sup> The following people and organisations endorsed parties and candidates:
</p>
<dl><dt>National Party</dt></dl>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Mark_Richardson_(cricketer)" title="Mark Richardson (cricketer)">Mark Richardson</a>, radio and television host and former Black Cap<sup id="cite_ref-184" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-184">&#91;178&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<dl><dt>Labour Party</dt></dl>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Jeremy_Corbyn" title="Jeremy Corbyn">Jeremy Corbyn</a>, leader of the <a href="/wiki/Labour_Party_(UK)" title="Labour Party (UK)">British Labour Party</a><sup id="cite_ref-185" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-185">&#91;179&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Dick_Hubbard" title="Dick Hubbard">Dick Hubbard</a>, former Mayor of Auckland<sup id="cite_ref-186" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-186">&#91;180&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Hollie_Smith" title="Hollie Smith">Hollie Smith</a>, singer<sup id="cite_ref-187" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-187">&#91;181&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/David_Tua" title="David Tua">David Tua</a>, boxer<sup id="cite_ref-188" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-188">&#91;182&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Guy_Williams_(comedian)" title="Guy Williams (comedian)">Guy Williams</a>, comedian<sup id="cite_ref-newshub.co.nz_189-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-newshub.co.nz-189">&#91;183&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Anika_Moa" title="Anika Moa">Anika Moa</a>, singer<sup id="cite_ref-190" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-190">&#91;184&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Taika_Waititi" title="Taika Waititi">Taika Waititi</a>, filmmaker<sup id="cite_ref-191" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-191">&#91;185&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<dl><dt>Green Party</dt></dl>
<ul><li>Gordon McLauchlan, author<sup id="cite_ref-192" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-192">&#91;186&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Guy_Williams_(comedian)" title="Guy Williams (comedian)">Guy Williams</a>, comedian<sup id="cite_ref-newshub.co.nz_189-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-newshub.co.nz-189">&#91;183&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<dl><dt><a href="/wiki/Epsom_(New_Zealand_electorate)" title="Epsom (New Zealand electorate)">Epsom</a> – <a href="/wiki/David_Seymour_(politician)" title="David Seymour (politician)">David Seymour</a> for ACT</dt></dl>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Bill_English" title="Bill English">Bill English</a>, Prime Minister and leader of National Party<sup id="cite_ref-ACTUF_193-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ACTUF-193">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<dl><dt><a href="/wiki/%C5%8Ch%C4%81riu_(New_Zealand_electorate)" title="Ōhāriu (New Zealand electorate)">Ōhāriu</a> – <a href="/wiki/Peter_Dunne" title="Peter Dunne">Peter Dunne</a> for United Future (until his resignation on 21 August 2017)</dt></dl>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Bill_English" title="Bill English">Bill English</a><sup id="cite_ref-ACTUF_193-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ACTUF-193">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>Brett Hudson, National Party candidate for Ōhāriu<sup id="cite_ref-194" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-194">&#91;188&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Opinion_polling">Opinion polling</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Zealand_general_election,_2017&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Opinion polling">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Opinion_polling_for_the_New_Zealand_general_election,_2017" title="Opinion polling for the New Zealand general election, 2017">Opinion polling for the New Zealand general election, 2017</a></div>
<p>Opinion polls have been undertaken periodically since the 2014 election, primarily by <a href="/wiki/MediaWorks_New_Zealand" title="MediaWorks New Zealand">MediaWorks New Zealand</a>, <a href="/wiki/Roy_Morgan_Research" title="Roy Morgan Research">Roy Morgan Research</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Television_New_Zealand" class="mw-redirect" title="Television New Zealand">Television New Zealand</a>. The graph on the left below shows the collated results of all polls for parties that polled above the 5% electoral threshold at the 2014 election; the graph on the right shows results for parties that polled between 1% and 4.9%, or won an electorate seat, at the 2014 election.
</p>
<center>
<table class="wikitable">

<tbody><tr>
<td><a href="/wiki/File:NZ_opinion_polls_2014-2017-majorparties.png" class="image"><img alt="NZ opinion polls 2014-2017-majorparties.png" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/NZ_opinion_polls_2014-2017-majorparties.png/450px-NZ_opinion_polls_2014-2017-majorparties.png" width="450" height="282" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/NZ_opinion_polls_2014-2017-majorparties.png/675px-NZ_opinion_polls_2014-2017-majorparties.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/80/NZ_opinion_polls_2014-2017-majorparties.png 2x" data-file-width="778" data-file-height="487" /></a>
</td>
<td><a href="/wiki/File:NZ_opinion_polls_2014-2017-minorparties.png" class="image"><img alt="NZ opinion polls 2014-2017-minorparties.png" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/NZ_opinion_polls_2014-2017-minorparties.png/450px-NZ_opinion_polls_2014-2017-minorparties.png" width="450" height="282" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/NZ_opinion_polls_2014-2017-minorparties.png/675px-NZ_opinion_polls_2014-2017-minorparties.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/8f/NZ_opinion_polls_2014-2017-minorparties.png 2x" data-file-width="778" data-file-height="487" /></a>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
</center>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Seat_predictions">Seat predictions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Zealand_general_election,_2017&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Seat predictions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The use of <a href="/wiki/Mixed-member_proportional_representation" title="Mixed-member proportional representation">mixed-member proportional representation</a> allows ready conversion of a party's support into a party vote percentage and therefore a number of seats in Parliament. Projections generally assume no changes to electorate seats each party holds (ACT retains Epsom, Māori retains Waiariki, Labour retains Te Tai Tokerau, etc.) unless there is a specific reason to assume change. For example, after <a href="/wiki/Peter_Dunne" title="Peter Dunne">Peter Dunne</a> announced his retirement, projections stopped assuming United Future would retain Ōhāriu. Other parties that do not pass the 5% threshold are assumed to not to win an electorate and therefore gain no seats.
</p><p>Radio New Zealand takes a "poll of polls" average to produce their forecast. The <i>New Zealand Herald</i> bases theirs on a predictive model incorporating poll data as well as past election results and past poll accuracy.<sup id="cite_ref-195" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-195">&#91;189&#93;</a></sup> Newshub and 1 News and produce projections based on their own polls only.
</p><p>When determining the scenarios for the overall result, the minimum parties necessary to form majority governments are listed (provided parties have indicated openness to working together). Actual governments formed may include other parties beyond the minimum required for a majority. This happened after the <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand_general_election,_2014" title="New Zealand general election, 2014">2014 election</a>, when National only needed one seat from another party to reach a 61-seat majority, but they formed a 64-seat government with Māori, ACT and United Future.
</p>

Taking into account that the polling average have three significant figures it is not possible to decide which party get the last seat. </span>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Post-election_events">Post-election events</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Zealand_general_election,_2017&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Post-election events">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Leadership_changes">Leadership changes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Zealand_general_election,_2017&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Leadership changes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Green_Party_of_Aotearoa_New_Zealand_female_co-leadership_election,_2018" title="Green Party of Aotearoa New Zealand female co-leadership election, 2018">Green Party of Aotearoa New Zealand female co-leadership election, 2018</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Metiria_Turei" title="Metiria Turei">Metiria Turei</a> stepped down as co-leader of the Green Party during the campaign period and the party did not elect a replacement before the election.<sup id="cite_ref-Co-Leader_resigns_204-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Co-Leader_resigns-204">&#91;196&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Local_by-elections">Local by-elections</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Zealand_general_election,_2017&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Local by-elections">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The following <a href="/wiki/Local_government_in_New_Zealand" title="Local government in New Zealand">local by-elections</a> were required due to the resignation of an incumbent local body politician following their election to Parliament:
</p>
<ul><li><b>Hastings by-elections, <a href="/wiki/Hastings_District" class="mw-redirect" title="Hastings District">Hastings District Council</a>:</b> Two by-elections were held in Hastings in 2017 after the resignations of Mayor <a href="/wiki/Lawrence_Yule" title="Lawrence Yule">Lawrence Yule</a> who was elected MP for <a href="/wiki/Tukituki_(New_Zealand_electorate)" title="Tukituki (New Zealand electorate)">Tukituki</a> and councillor Adrienne Pierce, who stood down from the council in August to contest <a href="/wiki/Palmerston_North_(New_Zealand_electorate)" title="Palmerston North (New Zealand electorate)">Palmerston North</a> for National.<sup id="cite_ref-205" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-205">&#91;197&#93;</a></sup> Following the election to the mayoralty of sitting Councillor and Deputy Mayor Sandra Hazlehurst, a subsequent by-election will be held in 2018.<sup id="cite_ref-206" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-206">&#91;198&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/List_of_by-elections_to_the_Wellington_City_Council#2017_by-election,_Southern_Ward" title="List of by-elections to the Wellington City Council">Southern Ward by-election</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wellington_City_Council" title="Wellington City Council">Wellington City Council</a>:</b> Deputy Mayor of Wellington <a href="/wiki/Paul_Eagle" title="Paul Eagle">Paul Eagle</a> resigned his seat on the council after he was elected MP for <a href="/wiki/Rongotai_(New_Zealand_electorate)" title="Rongotai (New Zealand electorate)">Rongotai</a>, necessitating a council by-election in the Southern Ward.<sup id="cite_ref-207" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-207">&#91;199&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><b>BOI–Whangaroa Ward by-election, <a href="/wiki/Far_North_District_Council" class="mw-redirect" title="Far North District Council">Far North District Council</a>:</b> Councillor <a href="/wiki/Willow-Jean_Prime" title="Willow-Jean Prime">Willow-Jean Prime</a> resigned to take her place as a Labour Party List MP. The by-election to replace her will be held in February 2018.<sup id="cite_ref-208" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-208">&#91;200&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><b>Auckland by-elections, <a href="/wiki/Auckland_Council" title="Auckland Council">Auckland Council</a>:</b> By-elections will be held in 2018 to fill vacancies on the Maungakiekie-Tamaki Ward and the Manurewa Local Board when <a href="/wiki/Denise_Lee" title="Denise Lee">Denise Lee</a> and <a href="/wiki/Simeon_Brown" title="Simeon Brown">Simeon Brown</a> were elected as MP for <a href="/wiki/Maungakiekie_(New_Zealand_electorate)" title="Maungakiekie (New Zealand electorate)">Maungakiekie</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pakuranga_(New_Zealand_electorate)" title="Pakuranga (New Zealand electorate)">Pakuranga</a>, respectively.<sup id="cite_ref-209" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-209">&#91;201&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Electoral_offences">Electoral offences</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Zealand_general_election,_2017&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Electoral offences">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>On 2 October 2017, the <a href="/wiki/Electoral_Commission_(New_Zealand)" title="Electoral Commission (New Zealand)">Electoral Commission</a> referred four alleged electoral offences to <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand_Police" title="New Zealand Police">Police</a>. These all involved social media posts of electoral statements during the <a href="/wiki/Election_silence" title="Election silence">election silence</a> period of midnight to 7:00pm on election day – a breach of section 197(1)(g)(i) of the Electoral Act. Two of these incidents concerned <a href="/wiki/Sean_Plunket" title="Sean Plunket">Sean Plunket</a>, the communications director of <a href="/wiki/The_Opportunities_Party" title="The Opportunities Party">The Opportunities Party</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-210" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-210">&#91;202&#93;</a></sup> On 18 October 2017, the Electoral Commission referred a subsequent offence to Police about <a href="/wiki/Television_New_Zealand" class="mw-redirect" title="Television New Zealand">TVNZ</a> re-broadcasting a bulletin of <i><a href="/wiki/Te_Karere" title="Te Karere">Te Karere</a></i> during the silence period.<sup id="cite_ref-211" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-211">&#91;203&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
